URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS154299298	2017-05-15 17:30:19	32408	species	Hooded Oriole	Icterus cucullatus			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33	USFWS_591		Desert NWR--Corn Creek (Field Station)	L168656	H	36.4387970	-115.3595660	2012-05-05	05:30:00	obsr235247	S10838974	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	420	3.219		2	1		0	1	0		Experienced an absolutely amazing migrant fallout on 5/5 at Corn Creek just north of Las Vegas.  David Donsker and I arrived at Corn Creek at first light and immediately noticed a number of kingbirds flying into the trees from the desert.  As the steady stream of birds continued, we noticed numerous warblers flying in along with the kingbirds.  The first tree we reached was loaded with Western Kingbirds, although only 10 feet tall it had at least 50 kingbirds on it perched like Christmas tree ornaments.  Each binocular field in each mulberry tree contained at least 5 Bullock's Orioles and a few more Western Kingbirds.  Groups of 5 to 10 warblers (mostly Audubon's) were in the trees, on the ground, in the grass, on fence posts - just everywhere.  We birded the oasis from 5:15 to 11 and conservatively estimated 2,000 birds of 75 species.  Counting was next to impossible as the birds were constantly flitting from tree to tree.  We returned and birded the area again from 2:30 to 5:30, and although activity was much lower, there were still hundreds of migrants, several more still flying in from the desert, and we found several species we didn't have in the morning.   Also saw 1 hybrid Eur Collared-Dove X Mourning Dove but could not determine how to enter this into e-bird.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS321403493	2019-01-31 15:58:23	32408	species	Hooded Oriole	Icterus cucullatus			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003		33	USFWS_591		Desert NWR--Corn Creek (Field Station)	L168656	H	36.4387970	-115.3595660	2015-05-19	05:52:00	obsr7422	S23543238	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	142	0.805		3	1	G1280396	0	1	0		This list records the birds encountered by Bob Barnes (Ridgecrest, Kern County, California), Paul Fox (Pacific Palisades, Los Angeles County, California), and Gerry Hawkins (Arlington, Virginia) during a relatively brief early morning visit to Desert National Wildlife Refuge-Corn Creek (Field Station) primarily to look for vagrants at the start of a 3/4 day transect from the desert well up into the Spring Mountains via Lee Canyon to the ski area, the NV Hwy 158 canyon Lee Canyon and Kyle Canyon connector road, and Kyle Canyon including walks at at the junction of Lee Canyon road and the road to Macks group campground, lower and upper Lee Meadows, lower and upper Bristlecone Trail, the paved trail across from Deer Creek picnic area, and Fletcher Canyon Trail. Highlights at Corn Creek included a lengthy experience with an incredibly well seen and heard male Rose-breasted Grosbeak (first called out by Gerry Hawkins) plus a seen and heard singing Bell's Vireo (first called out by Bob Barnes) and at least 50 Western Tanagers.  New Nevada birds for this final day: Sharp-shinned Hawk (Gerry Hawkins only), Sora (Paul Fox only), definitive Anna's Hummingbird, Rose-breasted Grosbeak, and Hooded Oriole (Bob Barnes only). This Corn Creek stop was the start of the 8th and final full day of birding for this southern Nevada birding trip to Clark County Las Vegas area and Lincoln County, Nevada birding sites. URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS205871853	2016-07-13 13:27:16	32408	species	Hooded Oriole	Icterus cucullatus			2			Male, Adult (1); Female, Adult (1)	United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Washoe	US-NV-031		9			Reno	L871332	P	39.5237399	-119.8119164	2013-07-25	15:45:00	obsr209998	S14787314	Incidental	P20	EBIRD				2	0		0	1	1			An adult male and female were seen coming to a hummingbird feeder off Tappan Dr. in Northwest Reno.  They both came into the hummingbird feeder three times while we were present.  Bob Russman and his wife (the owners of the home) observed the birds at about 11:00 am and about every 15-30 minutes after that when they came into the feeder.  A large thunderstorm did interrupt their feeding for a bit, but they did return once the downpour let up.  I observed the birds just a little before 4:00 pm until about 4:10 pm with a total observation time of about 2-3 minutes.  The male was clearly of the western race (nelsoni) and was very bright yellow with minimal orange.  The male's back was nearly entirely black with some pale edges, it had a large black throat that extended onto the upper breast, bold white wing bars and a decurved black bill with a bluish base to the lower mandible.  The female was mostly a pale yellow-green with a similar bill and thin white wing bars.  The birds were clearly a pair and stayed with each other during their visits to the yard.  Bob photographed the male and posted it to the NVbirds list serve.

Shrub in bloom, and oriole appeared to be eating the blossoms. I saw no eye mark, no eye mask. Did have dark beak and noticeably black mask starting above beak, extending down along throat into the chest area. Main body color yellow with an slight orangish tinge, perhaps. In appearance close to Sibley Guide to birds page 520 of non-breeding western hooded oriole.  As I said, not 100% but I am familiar with Bullocks and Baltimore. I knew immediately it was not a Baltimore. I did not see the eye mark of a Bullock's. The longer, slender black beak differentiates this bird from other colorful yellow-orange species, as does the size. Regret sighting too quick to capture with photo. I see that Bullock's and Baltimores can interbreed, so perhaps this is a X and not a hooded. But the Hooded pics came closest to what I observed.
